## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

# JCE Cryptography Provider Architecture Audit

## Your Task

Audit the Java Cryptography Extension (JCE) provider architecture in OpenJDK. Find: 1. The file `src/java.base/share/classes/java/security/Provider.java` that defines the abstract `Provider` class and its inner `Service` class. 2. The file that defines `Security` class which manages registered providers (`src/java.base/share/classes/java/security/Security.java`). 3. The SunJCE provider implementation file (`src/java.crypto/share/classes/com/sun/crypto/provider/SunJCE.java` or under `java.base`). 4. The file that implements `Cipher.getInstance()` — the factory method that resolves providers (`src/java.base/share/classes/javax/crypto/Cipher.java`). 5. The `java.security` configuration file that lists default providers. Report each file path and key class/method.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the security domain.

## Available Resources

No local repositories are pre-checked out.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/jdk--742e735d` (openjdk/jdk)

## Output Format

Create a file at `/workspace/answer.json` with your findings in the following structure:

```json
{
  "files": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go"}
  ],
  "symbols": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "SymbolName"}
  ],
  "chain": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "FunctionName"}
  ],
  "text": "Narrative explanation of your findings, citing repos and file paths."
}
```

Include only the fields relevant to this task. Your answer is evaluated against a closed-world oracle — completeness matters.

62-assistanttext-
Perfect! I have completed a comprehensive audit of the Java Cryptography Extension (JCE) provider architecture in OpenJDK. Here's a summary of what I found:

## Key Findings

I successfully located and analyzed **5 core components**:

### 1. **Provider.java** (Abstract Base Class)
   - Defines the `Provider` abstract class
   - Contains inner `Service` class (line 1500) - represents a cryptographic service
   - Methods: `putService()`, `getService()`, `getServices()`

### 2. **Security.java** (Provider Manager)
   - Manages registered security providers globally
   - Loads default providers from `java.security` configuration file
   - Key methods:
     - `addProvider()` - Registers new providers
     - `removeProvider()` - Removes providers
     - `getProviders()` - Returns all providers in preference order
     - `getProvider()` - Gets specific provider by name

### 3. **Cipher.java** (Factory Method)
   - `getInstance(String transformation)` method (line 538)
   - Implements sophisticated provider resolution:
     - Tokenizes transformation string (algorithm/mode/padding)
     - Generates multiple lookup variants for fallback
     - Searches registered providers in order
     - Instantiates CipherSpi from first matching service
     - Returns Cipher with lazy provider selection

### 4. **SunJCE.java** (Default Provider)
   - Extends Provider class
   - Constructor registers all supported cipher algorithms and modes
   - Uses `putEntries()` to register services with attributes like `SupportedModes` and `SupportedPaddings`
   - Singleton instance pattern

### 5. **java.security** (Configuration File)
   - Located at `src/java.base/share/conf/security/java.security`
   - Lists default providers (SUN, SunRsaSign, SunEC, SunJSSE, **SunJCE**, etc.)
   - Configures provider 
... (truncated)

A transformation always includes the name of a cryptographic\n63:  * algorithm (e.g., <i>AES</i>), and may be followed by a feedback mode and\n64:  * padding scheme.\n65:  *\n66:  * <p> A transformation is of the form:\n67:  *\n68:  * <ul>\n69:  * <li>\"<i>algorithm/mode/padding</i>\" or\n70:  *\n71:  * <li>\"<i>algorithm</i>\"\n72:  * </ul>\n73:  *\n74:  * <P> (in the latter case,\n75:  * provider-specific default values for the mode and padding scheme are used).\n76:  * For example, the following is a valid transformation:\n77:  *\n78:  * <pre>\n79:  *     Cipher c = Cipher.getInstance(\"<i>AES/CBC/PKCS5Padding</i>\");\n80:  * </pre>\n81:  *\n82:  * Using modes such as {@code CFB} and {@code OFB}, block\n83:  * ciphers can encrypt data in units smaller than the cipher's actual\n84:  * block size.  When requesting such a mode, you may optionally specify\n85:  * the number of bits to be processed at a time by appending this number\n86:  * to the mode name as shown in the \"{@code AES/CFB8/NoPadding}\" and\n87:  * \"{@code AES/OFB32/PKCS5Padding}\" transformations. If no such\n88:  * number is specified, a provider-specific default is used.\n89:  * (See the\n90:  * {@extLink security_guide_jdk_providers JDK Providers Documentation}\n91:  * for the JDK Providers default values.)\n92:  * Thus, block ciphers can be turned into byte-oriented stream ciphers by\n93:  * using an 8 bit mode such as CFB8 or OFB8.\n94:  * <p>\n95:  * Modes such as Authenticated Encryption with Associated Data (AEAD)\n96:  * provide authenticity assurances for both confidential data and\n97:  * Additional Associated Data (AAD) that is not encrypted.  (Please see\n98:  * <a href=\"http://www.ietf.org/rfc/rfc5116.txt\"> RFC 5116 </a> for more\n99:  * information on AEAD and AAD algorithms such as GCM/CCM.) Both\n100:  * confidential and AAD data can be used when calculating the\n101: ",
